  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_4088e614-6a79-4871-bf99-f3db353675af'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer16802383509952439073">A coterie of artists, prostitutes, saints, and seers are all aspiring towards fame and hoping for love and acceptance. Instead they find high rents, faithless partners, and dead-end careers. Offbeat, funny and bitingly satirical, "Slaves of New York" sheds an incomparable light on the city's denizens and social mores.</span>

<div id="review_1074665499" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1074665499" />
    <a title="Jonathan Ashleigh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33737465-jonathan-ashleigh"><img alt="Jonathan Ashleigh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1416341418p2/33737465.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1074665499?book_show_action=true">Oct 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan Ashleigh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan Ashleigh" href="/user/show/33737465-jonathan-ashleigh">Jonathan Ashleigh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1074665499" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14204601946499665571">This was the first book of short stories I have ever read that felt like a novel at some points. The characters are interesting and the dialog seems real and the characters you come to know are people you want to know about. Marley and Elenor are the main ones and their stories only coincide for a moment, but the rest of everyone seems like they could all be living in the same building. Because this is a book about New York with "New York" in the title, there is something understood going in and</span>
  <span id="freeText14204601946499665571" style="display:none">This was the first book of short stories I have ever read that felt like a novel at some points. The characters are interesting and the dialog seems real and the characters you come to know are people you want to know about. Marley and Elenor are the main ones and their stories only coincide for a moment, but the rest of everyone seems like they could all be living in the same building. Because this is a book about New York with "New York" in the title, there is something understood going in and that is one of the reasons this feels novelesque even though some of the short stories/chapters are about far off topics told from varying perspectives. Many of the stories seem to connect anyway but the story about <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/236007.Bruce_Springsteen" title="Bruce Springsteen" rel="nofollow noopener">Bruce Springsteen</a> is one that I couldn't comprehend. It's a quick read and this book will probably rise to prominence again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14204601946499665571" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText1526777606342596641" style="display:none">Just finished it. So I'm 20 years after the fact. Sue me.<br /><br />Well, I liked it...but I'm sure that's at least partly a by-product of being old enough to remember New York in the 80s when artists (and junkies and drifters...sometimes combined in one body) could be found all over New York. It's a scattershot memoir of a time gone by; bittersweet for me. Hated the hair of the 80s; loved the experimentalism. The 80s took more chances than any decade of the century other than the 60s and, maybe, the 20s.<br /><br />Side note...I'm baffled by people who talk about this with <i>Sex in the City</i> in any way, shape, or form. The narrators/protagonists of Janowitz's book are avant garde artists and social fringe dwellers trying to stay off drugs and scrape together rent to stay in scummy apartments. They are virtually as far from Carrie and Co. as it's possible to be. Comparing this to SATC (or putting down one work in the context of the other) is like comparing <i>Peanuts</i> and <i>Watership Down</i> because they both deal with animals.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2016868360" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2016868360" />
    <a title="Matthew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20910065-matthew"><img alt="Matthew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587674435p2/20910065.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2016868360?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew" href="/user/show/20910065-matthew">Matthew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2016868360" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7342814280802397418">I had been looking for a book exactly like Tama Janowitz’s Slaves of New York. Not so much one made up of loosely connected short stories – though it’s an approach I’ll almost always find irresistible, even if executed haphazardly – or that romanticized an era which has always fascinated me (in this case, the early 1980’s). If anything, I was looking for the literary equivalent to Nan Goldin’s landmark photo exhibit, “The Ballad of Sexual Dependency”. What do you know, it’s been right under my n</span>
  <span id="freeText7342814280802397418" style="display:none">I had been looking for a book exactly like Tama Janowitz’s Slaves of New York. Not so much one made up of loosely connected short stories – though it’s an approach I’ll almost always find irresistible, even if executed haphazardly – or that romanticized an era which has always fascinated me (in this case, the early 1980’s). If anything, I was looking for the literary equivalent to Nan Goldin’s landmark photo exhibit, “The Ballad of Sexual Dependency”. What do you know, it’s been right under my nose for over thirty years. <br /><br />So, I guess I am late to the party. But boy oh boy am I happy I still made an appearance. Slaves of New York is nothing if not revolutionary, a masterful homage to an eclectic, hedonistic period – and the many eccentrics from which it sprung – long-lost yet forever etched in time. Janowitz documents a colorful cast of characters, ranging from artists, to models, to hookers, to junkies and beyond, as they navigate through their everyday lives on the streets and subways, and in the lofts, clubs, and galleries of NYC. They are why New York is often referred to as “the city that never sleeps”.<br /><br />Billed as short stories Slaves of New York reads more like a novel, with certain central characters – most notably struggling jewelry designer Eleanor, her artist boyfriend Stash and self-proclaimed genius Marley; even a cat named Snowball makes several cameos – either floating amidst the loose storyline peripherally, or acting as the predominant focal point. Janowitz’s satirical, distant style , not unlike that of her direct “brat pack” peers Bret Easton Ellis and Jay McInerney, offers a singular point of view that allows for each member of her ensemble to both stand out and blend in without ultimately sacrificing the narrative. On occasion Janowitz veers off into relatively uncharted territory – “case studies” on particular one-off characters; a fantastical tale of a groupie giving Bruce Springsteen’s wife a lobotomy – yet never to the point in which you feel as though you’re reading an entirely different collection. <br /><br />Several themes dominate Janowitz’s stories, most especially that of gender, particularly within the art scene. The author presents us with a world wherein men are allowed to behave badly and women are often considered accessories (or, worse, lucky to be dating certain men); and yet it’s the females that come off as heroic despite the traditional values of what’s typically considered a progressive society. This is best demonstrated by that of Eleanor, who goes from falling in love with Stash, to living with him, to relying upon him, to dropping him from her life, across several vignettes. She’s at first obliviously subservient, and then grows more and more independent as her relationship falters. And yet, as documented in “Matches”, she still feels empty without a male presence in her life; Eleanor, while remarkably self-aware, still has room to evolve. <br /><br />In fact, just about every one of Janowitz’s “slaves” does. Whether they are aware of this is an entirely other story, though. Many – Marley, for instance – believe they’ve already evolved (the men especially), that the world revolves around them and that all others within their orbit are just living in it. In reality they’re helpless and hopeless allusions. <br /><br />Or, more fittingly, they are slaves to the grind – albeit a rather fascinating one, and one I am thrilled to have discovered. Even if I am a bit late to the party. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7342814280802397418" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1841637786" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14406817189569304446">3.5 stars. This is a collection of tales about New York City in the 1980s and is considered a major work of the literary brat pack. The best thing about this was Janowitz’s writing because it’s incredibly unique and addictive, weaving through the lives of various people around New York and daring you to follow them in their hedonistic and self destructive tirades.<br/><br/>I liked some stories better than others; the very last story was my absolute favourite and has left me genuinely haunted. At some part</span>
  <span id="freeText14406817189569304446" style="display:none">3.5 stars. This is a collection of tales about New York City in the 1980s and is considered a major work of the literary brat pack. The best thing about this was Janowitz’s writing because it’s incredibly unique and addictive, weaving through the lives of various people around New York and daring you to follow them in their hedonistic and self destructive tirades.<br /><br />I liked some stories better than others; the very last story was my absolute favourite and has left me genuinely haunted. At some parts I found the stories quite stale but I think disillusionment and stagnation is part of the 80s youth culture so didn’t mind this. Reading so much Ellis and McInerney made me know what I was in for before I event started this.<br /><br />Tama is amazing anyway. Like she is just wonderful. I want to read more of her stuff and I think she’ll excel in her later novels and memoir. </span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13500603309663045729">Slaves of New York is made up of individual stories about people in the art scene in the 80s. The characters are very original, with some reccurring ones: Eleanor the jewelry designer, and Marley the "genius" painter.   <span id="freeText13500603309663045729" style="display:none">Slaves of New York is made up of individual stories about people in the art scene in the 80s. The characters are very original, with some reccurring ones: Eleanor the jewelry designer, and Marley the "genius" painter. For me, the downside was the length of this book made up of unrelated stories. I wasn't enthusiastic about continuing after the first half because I knew there wasn't going to be any character development or plot twists I could be excited about. There were only many peeks inside lives in New York, similar to the ones before them.<br />Although it's clear that talent is there, I'm not sure if Janowitz has put it to good use. I found it hard to finish the book, and I would abandon it if I was okay with not finishing books. It's a good read for a lazy afternoon and for a hundred pages. After that, not so much.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3286143551734805739">A collection of short stories centered on a few downtown NYC artists. Janowitz is not without talent, but too many of these stories feel almost extravagantly pointless, trailing off into a kind of deadpan absurdity or hipster poignancy (it took me over a month to finally finish it). I did enjoy the stories centered on Eleanor, a mixed up twenty-something jewelry maker involved in a bad relationship with a semi-successful artist named Stash; these seemed the most focused and relatable (not to men</span>
  <span id="freeText3286143551734805739" style="display:none">A collection of short stories centered on a few downtown NYC artists. Janowitz is not without talent, but too many of these stories feel almost extravagantly pointless, trailing off into a kind of deadpan absurdity or hipster poignancy (it took me over a month to finally finish it). I did enjoy the stories centered on Eleanor, a mixed up twenty-something jewelry maker involved in a bad relationship with a semi-successful artist named Stash; these seemed the most focused and relatable (not to mention—at their very best—sharply witty and observant). Mostly this is an 80's curio, a snapshot of author Janowitz's 15 minutes in the mid-to-late years of that decade; the cover art alone is the 80's in a nutshell. </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer651632674" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2963338368312272564">I have had this book ever since it came out and couldn't sleep last night and pulled it off the shelf. Parts of this book have lived in my head since I first read it in the 1980's. I loved it then. I gobbled up this book in NYC at the time, visiting my brother who was living on Hudson Street. Tama put her finger on a time and place in NYC that is long gone, but still resonates.<br/>If you want to know what the city was like then, this is it. Read it and weep. She is all that and a bag of chips.<br/>When I</span>
  <span id="freeText2963338368312272564" style="display:none">I have had this book ever since it came out and couldn't sleep last night and pulled it off the shelf. Parts of this book have lived in my head since I first read it in the 1980's. I loved it then. I gobbled up this book in NYC at the time, visiting my brother who was living on Hudson Street. Tama put her finger on a time and place in NYC that is long gone, but still resonates.<br />If you want to know what the city was like then, this is it. Read it and weep. She is all that and a bag of chips.<br />When I first read this book, I envied her style and longed for her success. I wanted to have her career! This book still packs a punch and the moments I remembered are still strong. This was the first novel I ever read that depicted people eating live monkey brains. I never knew it existed. Still haven't forgotten those scenes...and remain haunted by them.<br />I am surprised Tama's become such a whipping post for critics. She still has an amazing turn of phrase. I still love her and will read some of her other stories next.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2963338368312272564" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_193433673" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/193433673" />
    <a title="Marie Irshad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5943233-marie-irshad"><img alt="Marie Irshad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1396976606p2/5943233.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/193433673?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marie Irshad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marie Irshad" href="/user/show/5943233-marie-irshad">Marie Irshad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330297530" href="/book/show/2205762.Slaves_Of_New_York">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer193433673" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11500512964967006688">As a teenager I was a little obsessed with the idea of American artists living in lofts in New York, so this collection of short stories really appealed to me. <br/><br/>I've not read it since but I have overwhelming memories of being irritated to hell by the character Eleanor, a hat designer who appears in several of the stories. She's an annoying drip stuck in a relationship with a selfish artist boyfriend who treats her like crap. I couldn't understand why she stayed with him. Not entirely sure if I'd </span>
  <span id="freeText11500512964967006688" style="display:none">As a teenager I was a little obsessed with the idea of American artists living in lofts in New York, so this collection of short stories really appealed to me. <br /><br />I've not read it since but I have overwhelming memories of being irritated to hell by the character Eleanor, a hat designer who appears in several of the stories. She's an annoying drip stuck in a relationship with a selfish artist boyfriend who treats her like crap. I couldn't understand why she stayed with him. Not entirely sure if I'd read it again although it does have one of the best first lines I've ever seen in a book. </span>

<div id="review_2361862243" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2361862243" />
    <a title="berthamason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10717517-berthamason"><img alt="berthamason" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588568686p2/10717517.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2361862243?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="berthamason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="berthamason" href="/user/show/10717517-berthamason">berthamason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671745240" href="/book/show/946776.Slaves_of_New_York">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2361862243" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17408180774166671076">I started <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/233504.Slaves_of_New_York' title='Slaves of New York by Tama Janowitz' rel='nofollow noopener'>Slaves of New York</a> after reading both Thomas DePietro’s and Jay McInerney’s reviews of the book. At first, I found their criticism harsh but as I delved into the book I ended up agreeing with most of the points made by them. I agree with McInerney’s remark that the characters in Janowitz’s short stories “do not experience catharsis or epiphany”, “stories are static” and the “maddening passivity” displayed by these characters is “reflected in the narrative stance”. <br/><br/>I found Eleanor parti</span>
  <span id="freeText17408180774166671076" style="display:none">I started <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/233504.Slaves_of_New_York" title="Slaves of New York by Tama Janowitz" rel="nofollow noopener">Slaves of New York</a> after reading both Thomas DePietro’s and Jay McInerney’s reviews of the book. At first, I found their criticism harsh but as I delved into the book I ended up agreeing with most of the points made by them. I agree with McInerney’s remark that the characters in Janowitz’s short stories “do not experience catharsis or epiphany”, “stories are static” and the “maddening passivity” displayed by these characters is “reflected in the narrative stance”. <br /><br />I found Eleanor particularly irritating in her cluelessness and passivity for two reasons: she gets more stories than other recurrent characters and she always seems to be on the verge of an epiphany that never happens. Her moments of quasi-insight frustrated me immensely throughout the book. Eleanor is capable of self-realisation - “I understood that I was at a period in my life when everything was falling apart” (260) – but she somehow always ends up reverting to her old passivity. <br /><br />The nonchalant narrative tone employed by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/58272.Tama_Janowitz" title="Tama Janowitz" rel="nofollow noopener">Tama Janowitz</a> suggests a lack of care for her characters that results in the reader caring even less for whatever happens to them. Examples: Eleanor (once again) repeating to herself that she’s “getting used to” (8) her boyfriend’s psychological abuse, deciding that her actions don’t make a difference - “If I had gotten into the limousine earlier that evening I’d be in the same mess, only in a different neighbourhood” (135) – or another character choosing “to ignore the problem” (147) instead of facing it.<br /><br />I also agree with DePietro’s observation that the reader is “never certain whether these often disposable tales are a symptom or a parody of the junk culture Janowitz chronicles so well”. Even after reading a few interviews with her and some excerpts from her autobiography I’m still not sure how I should approach her work. <br /><br />Was her intention to write satirical stories? If so, this would, at least in my opinion, elevate the literary status of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/233504.Slaves_of_New_York" title="Slaves of New York by Tama Janowitz" rel="nofollow noopener">Slaves of New York</a>. <br /><br />According to reviews, in her autobiography, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24331504.Scream_A_Memoir_of_Glamour_and_Dysfunction" title="Scream A Memoir of Glamour and Dysfunction by Tama Janowitz" rel="nofollow noopener">Scream: A Memoir of Glamour and Dysfunction</a>, she manages to reveal as little about herself as the characters in her stories: “When Janowitz gets coy or retreats into cliché in passages about her famous friends, it doesn’t seem to be out of discretion. You get the sense, rather, that it pains her to give readers the gossip they want”.<br /><br />When Berman concludes that the sentences in her autobiography “rarely have the emotional impact she intends” due to her “short, blunt, purposely self-evident” writing style, I can’t help but agree with him. <br /><br />I only wish I could have known her characters better.<br /><br />There’s a good interview on YouTube in which she discusses her autobiography and life in New York in the 1980s: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ew-rWldGhXc">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ew-rW...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="17408180774166671076" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_343861105" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/343861105" />
    <a title="Chuck" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9203818-chuck"><img alt="Chuck" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1335912215p2/9203818.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/343861105?book_show_action=true">Jun 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chuck" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chuck" href="/user/show/9203818-chuck">Chuck</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9203818-chuck?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer343861105" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3192670145948635114">I love short story books where the stories connect with each other. I've only read one other and that was "Adverbs" by David Handler. This one does the same thing, though it has a few short stories that are unrelated. They're sort of serial, but basically they all tell of the life and thoughts of people in the avante garde art scene in the 80's. I like how the author picked characters that were on the fringe of the scene, not ones that were completely submersed in it. In that same vein, I had a </span>
  <span id="freeText3192670145948635114" style="display:none">I love short story books where the stories connect with each other. I've only read one other and that was "Adverbs" by David Handler. This one does the same thing, though it has a few short stories that are unrelated. They're sort of serial, but basically they all tell of the life and thoughts of people in the avante garde art scene in the 80's. I like how the author picked characters that were on the fringe of the scene, not ones that were completely submersed in it. In that same vein, I had a hard time connecting to the characters, and it made their stories dull at the end. With Marley, the delusional artist, she did a good job at catching his egocentric personality through his narration, I was actually really impressed by it. I would suggest it as an example to anyone trying to write first person narrative for an egotistical, eccentric character. My one gripe with this book was that she put WAY too much emphasis on food and I didn't find it to be necessary. She should have gone languid details on the art, or even on the fashion, of the characters, but instead it was the food, which makes me wonder if she herself was a starving artist. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3192670145948635114" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1429788049" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1429788049" />
    <a title="Kirsty Bates" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48443843-kirsty-bates"><img alt="Kirsty Bates" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578669997p2/48443843.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1429788049?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kirsty Bates" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kirsty Bates" href="/user/show/48443843-kirsty-bates">Kirsty Bates</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48443843-kirsty-bates?shelf=women-s-blank-fiction">women-s-blank-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1429788049" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10470929266915408593">Janowitz's writing is very hip. Her stories are short, they're arguably trivial and even -- pointless, they deal with all manner of hipsters and under-appreciated artists and the hypocrisies surrounding trying to 'make it' in New York. I think to some extent, she tries to achieve a kind of, disaffected carelessness when it comes to presenting their stories. After all, the media later placed her well within the centre of the infamous literary 'brat-pack'. There's no denying either that Janowitz w</span>
  <span id="freeText10470929266915408593" style="display:none">Janowitz's writing is very hip. Her stories are short, they're arguably trivial and even -- pointless, they deal with all manner of hipsters and under-appreciated artists and the hypocrisies surrounding trying to 'make it' in New York. I think to some extent, she tries to achieve a kind of, disaffected carelessness when it comes to presenting their stories. After all, the media later placed her well within the centre of the infamous literary 'brat-pack'. There's no denying either that Janowitz wasn't an icon of downtown bohemia, and I think she's trying (perhaps too hard) to emulate this through her fiction as well. But for me, there's only one problem -- she's trying to write too loosely about characters she obviously cares a lot about. In fact, many readers spot the correlation between Eleanor's experiences, for instance, and Janowitz's own, creating the argument that a large portion of Slaves of New York is in fact, autobiographical, which in turn, counteracts this notion of 'distance' that Janowitz is trying so hard to create.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10470929266915408593" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/871594330" />
    <a title="Rachel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17358486-rachel"><img alt="Rachel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374774099p2/17358486.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/871594330?book_show_action=true">Mar 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel" href="/user/show/17358486-rachel">Rachel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17358486-rachel?shelf=s">s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17358486-rachel?shelf=female-author">female-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer871594330" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5449852557311767140">This is very much a novel of its time set in the consumerist 80's and it hasn't aged as well as others written around a similar time. Many of the characters as selfish and self-centred, focusing on where their next hit, drink or sexual encounter would come from. <br/><br/>The book itself is written as a set of short interlinking stories. Some of the characters appear more than once and others do not. Normally I like this style of writing as it makes it easy to pick up and put down when time is short, but </span>
  <span id="freeText5449852557311767140" style="display:none">This is very much a novel of its time set in the consumerist 80's and it hasn't aged as well as others written around a similar time. Many of the characters as selfish and self-centred, focusing on where their next hit, drink or sexual encounter would come from. <br /><br />The book itself is written as a set of short interlinking stories. Some of the characters appear more than once and others do not. Normally I like this style of writing as it makes it easy to pick up and put down when time is short, but most of these stories were on the monotonous and repetitive side and I often felt that Janowitz was trying to compete too much with people like Bret Easton Ellis, who pull of this style of story much better.<br /><br />New York is normally a place that I enjoy reading about but the descriptions in this did little for me - maybe because they had been done better elsewhere.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5449852557311767140" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75351161" />
    <a title="Godzilla" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/737004-godzilla"><img alt="Godzilla" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1221722692p2/737004.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/75351161?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Godzilla" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Godzilla" href="/user/show/737004-godzilla">Godzilla</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330297530" href="/book/show/2205762.Slaves_Of_New_York">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/737004-godzilla?shelf=life-but-not-as-we-know-it">life-but-not-as-we-know-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/737004-godzilla?shelf=2011">2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer75351161" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2994752889810057673">I had fonder memories of this that my re-reading discovered.<br/><br/>If ever there was a book of it's time, this may well be it.<br/><br/>The characters are shallow and neurotic, and the stories feel garbled and chaotic.<br/><br/>There are some sharply observed moments of satire in here, but it's really like panning for gold: the odd glimmer isn't going to totally enrich your life.<br/><br/>i found it a chore at times to finish reading it, and were it not for my OCD about finishing books I start, I may not have got through all this a</span>
  <span id="freeText2994752889810057673" style="display:none">I had fonder memories of this that my re-reading discovered.<br /><br />If ever there was a book of it's time, this may well be it.<br /><br />The characters are shallow and neurotic, and the stories feel garbled and chaotic.<br /><br />There are some sharply observed moments of satire in here, but it's really like panning for gold: the odd glimmer isn't going to totally enrich your life.<br /><br />i found it a chore at times to finish reading it, and were it not for my OCD about finishing books I start, I may not have got through all this again.<br /><br />Another sharp lesson from life, some things are best left in the past. I'm left questioning myself as to why I've held onto this book through several house moves and a number of years!</span>

<div id="review_1255525083" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1255525083" />
    <a title="Nicholas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/646385-nicholas"><img alt="Nicholas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1305402161p2/646385.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1255525083?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicholas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicholas" href="/user/show/646385-nicholas">Nicholas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1255525083" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15103090022145264360">Slaves of New York seems best read as an artifact from New York's art world in the 1980s. It had occasional moments of humor, but more of just flat out absurdity. I don't think Janowitz intends for us to actually know any of the characters or necessarily find their predicaments particularly believable. She writes about the outlandish in order to poke fun at the foolishness and pretensions of denizens of New York's art scene. All that said, I also just didn't find it all that much fun, even thoug</span>
  <span id="freeText15103090022145264360" style="display:none">Slaves of New York seems best read as an artifact from New York's art world in the 1980s. It had occasional moments of humor, but more of just flat out absurdity. I don't think Janowitz intends for us to actually know any of the characters or necessarily find their predicaments particularly believable. She writes about the outlandish in order to poke fun at the foolishness and pretensions of denizens of New York's art scene. All that said, I also just didn't find it all that much fun, even though it was certainly quick. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15103090022145264360" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_94545245" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/94545245" />
    <a title="kate rushing" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1395564-kate-rushing"><img alt="kate rushing" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1439217483p2/1395564.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="kate rushing" class="user" itemprop="url" name="kate rushing" href="/user/show/1395564-kate-rushing">kate rushing</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer94545245" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16084027404601548897">"you and bruce" is my favorite. all of them are very shallow. at some points you can't tell whether the overt stupidity and mistrust of the reader comes from the narrator or the author. i don't recommend reading all of them - it's like eating so much ice cream that your mouth gets cold and you can't even taste anymore. when i finished the book i wanted to go sit in some dumb, lush romance novel where all the characters have feelings, banal though they may be.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer705432592" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11179418027155015174">I have loved the movie since I was in High School before I subsquintly moved to New York where I started my own life as an artist. I finally just started reading the original book and it is great. My goal was for my book Getting Personal to be a more up to date LA version with the same unapologetic rawness. Definately read Slaves of New York, and watch the movie if you can. It's a who's who of great Indie film actors before they blew up.</span>

          </span>

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('TW5XSf3ZOlH5yt4w5qrYWvstb9Gpfoaq/SShuscbXw8r7AgyRvoDrmFCttJlfvLMbRc2ugABnqZqiCpKX+ZDAw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('kX9nqXZSFwmGAPF/9AHeZUyfeep2v7x+pw0RGwhGnoj3/TjSzXEu9h6ImZ131fTz2qUggd/ApHIwoZrrkLuChA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/58272.Tama_Janowitz">About Tama Janowitz</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11868739124154495907">Tama Janowitz is an American novelist and a short story writer. The 2005 September/October issue of Pages magazine listed her as one of the four "brat pack" authors, along with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2751.Bret_Easton_Ellis' title='Bret Easton Ellis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Bret Easton Ellis</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/60729.Mark_Lindquist' title='Mark Lindquist' rel='nofollow noopener'>Mark Lindquist</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14079.Jay_McInerney' title='Jay McInerney' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jay McInerney</a>.<br/><br/>Born in San Francisco, California to a psychiatrist father and literature professor mother who divorced when she was ten, Janowitz moved to the East Coast of the United St</span>
  <span id="freeText11868739124154495907" style="display:none">Tama Janowitz is an American novelist and a short story writer. The 2005 September/October issue of Pages magazine listed her as one of the four "brat pack" authors, along with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2751.Bret_Easton_Ellis" title="Bret Easton Ellis" rel="nofollow noopener">Bret Easton Ellis</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/60729.Mark_Lindquist" title="Mark Lindquist" rel="nofollow noopener">Mark Lindquist</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14079.Jay_McInerney" title="Jay McInerney" rel="nofollow noopener">Jay McInerney</a>.<br /><br />Born in San Francisco, California to a psychiatrist father and literature professor mother who divorced when she was ten, Janowitz moved to the East Coast of the United States to attend Barnard College and the Columbia University School of the Arts and started writing about life in New York City, where she had settled down.<br /><br />She socialized with Andy Warhol and became well-known in New York's literary and social circles. Her 1986 collection of short stories, Slaves of New York brought her wider fame. Slaves of New York was adapted into a 1989 film directed by James Ivory and starring Bernadette Peters. Janowitz wrote the screenplay and also appeared, playing Peters' friend.<br /><br />Janowitz has published seven novels, one collection of stories and one work of non-fiction. She lives in Brooklyn with her husband, Tim Hunt, and their adopted daughter.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11868739124154495907" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
